Danish Technological Institute.
The project originates from the innovation project "Green
Concrete ll" which aims to create a green transition of cement and concrete production in
Denmark. The project has been written in the period between the 1st of August 2016 to the
27th of January 2017 and counts for 35 ECTS points. A pre-project corresponding to 15 ECTS
points was completed prior to the thesis, in the spring of 2016.

IV
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Concrete structures build in colder climates are often subjected to freezing temperatures and
salts, either in the form of de-icers or sea water. The combination is known to be a durability
problem and can cause a premature end to the service life. A project conducted by the Danish
Technological Institute showed issues with the frost resistance for concrete with high contents of
ﬂy ash. Fly ash can be used as a cement replacement and is often added due its positive eﬀect
on the environmental proﬁle. It is therefore an issue if a suﬃcient durability can not be reach
regarding frost action.
Two hypotheses concerning the poor frost resistance for concrete with ﬂy ash are devolved and
investigated separately. The ﬁrst hypothesis concerns the air void analyses and suggest that the
results for concrete containing ﬂy ash is misleading and gives a false impression of a suﬃcient
air void distribution. The other hypothesis concerns the surface properties of the test specimens
and focuses on changes due to the two damaging mechanisms, leaching and bicarbonation. The
hypotheses will be investigated by casting concrete and paste specimens with increasing content
of ﬂy ash and subjecting the specimens to various tests.
Air void analyses conducted on paste specimens proved that cenospheres in the ﬂy ash are
registered as air voids making the air void distribution appear more beneﬁcial than actual the
case. However, after adjusting for the eﬀect of the hollow ﬂy ash grains, none of the tested
concrete specimens showed spacing factors above the critical limit. Frost tests conducted to a
concrete with a high content of ﬂy ash and an extra high content of air entraining agent showed
large improvements to the frost resistance. The results indicate that concrete with ﬂy ash might
have a lower critical spacing factor than regular cement concrete. The need for extra air could
be attributed to the quality of the surface. The Microscopic analysis proved that changes occur
in the exposed surface where bicarbonation is formed and the chemical composition is changed.
If the quality is compromised as a result of the changes the necessary air content might need to
be increased for the concrete to be durable.
V
==PAGE:6==
Sammenfatning
Beton konstruktioner opført i kolde klimaer er often eksponeret for frost grader og salte, enten i
form af tømiddel eller saltvand. Kombinationen er kendt for at være et problem for holdbarheden
og kan medføre for tidlig slut på levetiden. Et projekt udført af Teknoloigsk Institut viste prob-
lemer med frost bestandigheden for beton med højt indhold af ﬂyveaske. Flyveaske kan bruges
som cement erstatning og er ofte anvendt grundet den positive eﬀect på miljøproﬁlen. Det er der-
for et problem hvis den nødvendige holdbarhed ikke kan opnås med hensyn til frost bestandighed.
To hypoteser vedrørerende the lave frost bestandighed for beton med højt indhold af ﬂyve aske
er udviklet og undersøgt seperat. Den første hypotese omhandler luft pore analysen og foreslår
at resultaterne for beton med ﬂyve aske er misvisende og giver et falsk indtryk af en tilstrækkelig
luft pore fordeling.
Den anden hypotese omhandler overﬂade egenskaberne for test emnerne
og fokuserer på ændringer grundet de to skades mekanismer, udludning og bikarbonatisering.
Hypoteserne vil blive undersøgt ved at støbe beton og pasta prøver med stigende indhold af
ﬂyve aske og derefter udsætte emnerne for en række tests.
Luftpore analysen udført på pasta prøver viste at cenosfærer i ﬂyveasken bliver registreret som
luftpore hvilket får luftpore fordelingen til at virke mere gavnlig end det egentlig er tilfældet.
Men efter en justerering for eﬀekten af hule ﬂyve aske korn, viste ingen af betonerne en afstands-
faktor over grænseværdien. Frost tests udført på beton med højt indhold af ﬂyve aske and ekstra
højt indhold af luft indblandingsmiddel viste store forbedringer på frost bestandigheden. Resul-
taterne indikerede at beton med ﬂyveaske kan have en lavere kritisk afstandsfaktor end regulær
beton. Behovet for ekstra luft kan muligvis være forbundet med kvaliteten af overﬂaden. Den
mikroskopiske analyse beviste at der sker ændringer i den eksponerede overﬂade hvor bikarbona-
tisering bliver dannet og den kemiske sammensætning ændres. Hvis kvaliteten er komprimenteret
som resultat af ænderingerne, bliver behovet for luft muligvis øget, for at betonen fortsat skal
have acceptable holdbarhed.

Introduction
Concrete is the most commonly used building material in the world and does therefore have a
signiﬁcant eﬀect on a series of environmental parameters. The concrete industry in Denmark
has long been focused on reducing the environmental impact associated with the production of
concrete and cement. The production of cement alone is responsible for approximately 5 % of
the total global CO2 emission. One way to optimize the environmental proﬁle of concrete is to
replace part of the cement with biproducts from other industries. Some of these biproducts are
often referred to as supplementary cementitious materials, shortened SCM [24]. Since the 1970s,
ﬂy ash has been used as a SCM in Denmark. Today the use of either ﬂy ash or microsilica
are considered necessary to reach suﬃcient durability for concrete structures in aggressive
environments [16].
Despite the high CO2 emission associated with the cement production, concrete can still be
sustainable. If the concrete is properly designed, the structure can achieve a long service life
with a minimum need for maintenance compared with equivalent structures build with other
materials [24]. It is necessary to know which durability problems the concrete might be sub-
jected to when designing the mix in order to reach the optimum preformance of the material [25].
The Concrete Center at Danish Technological Institute has, as a part of the project Green Con-
crete II, developed diﬀerent concrete recipes with the purpose of improving the environmental
proﬁle. A series of concrete was developed with diﬀerent contents of ﬂy ash with replacement
levels up to 50 % relative to the cement mass.
If a concrete mix does not apply with the
guidelines stated in DS 2426, its durability, safety and strength should be demonstrated by
laboratory testing.
According to DS 2426:2011, a maximum ﬂy ash to cement ratio of 0.33
relative to the mass is allowed [8]. As the content of the developed concretes are higher than the
limit, the properties of the mix is tested to prove that they comply with the the values given in
the standards. For the durability documentation for Green Concrete II, freeze/thaw tests was
conducted according to DS/EN 12390-9 for the developed concretes. The test revealed issues
with salt frost scaling for the concrete containing high levels of replacement.
During the spring of 2016 a pre-project, corresponding to 15 ECTS points, was completed. The
background for the project was the freeze/thaw tests performed by DTI on ﬂy ash concretes.
The aim was to investigate the cause of the accelerated scaling for high contents of ﬂy ash. Five
concrete recipes with an increasing content of ﬂy ash was developed and tested in the laboratory
at DTI. The freeze/thaw tests showed an increase of scaling with the increase of ﬂy ash, even
though the compressive strength was signiﬁcantly higher for the concretes containing ﬂy ash and
they fulﬁlled the requirements regarding the air void structure.
1
==PAGE:10==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
The pre-project is detailed described in Appendix A. From the results two hypothesis was de-
veloped.
• Hollow ﬂy ash particles might be analysed as air voids in the air void analysis, giving the
impression of a suﬃcient air void structure for concretes with high contents of ﬂy ash.
However, as the air void structure is actually inadequate the result are extensive scaling.
All recipes was devolved with a target air content of 6 % +/- 1 % in the fresh concrete.
To reach the target air content, it would have been expected that the necessary addition
of air entraining agent would increase with the increase of the ﬂy ash content. However,
the opposite trend was observed, which supports the theory of misleading results from the
air void analysis.
• Bicarbonation might form in the concrete with ﬂy ash due to the low content of Ca(OH)2.
The bicarbonation can be a durability problem for concrete and is known to increase
the permeability and reduce the strength. It can also be a sign of the other damaging
mechanism, leaching. The scanning electron microscope analysis of the freeze/thaw exposed
specimens showed a formation of bicarbonation for all concretes containing ﬂy ash, whereas
bicarbonation was not present for the concrete without ﬂy ash.
This report will further address the issues with frost resistance for concrete containing high
amounts of ﬂy ash. The two hypotheses from the pre-project will be investigated and it will
be determined at which replacement level the content of ﬂy ash starts to become a durability
problem.
Katja Udbye Christensen

Theory
2.1
Portland Cement
Cement is one of the most used materials in the world. It acts like a glue, binding together
sand and stone in concrete. When cement is mixed with water an exotermic reation takes place
where heat is released [23].
Portland cement is a hydraulic binder, which according to ACI
CT-13 is deﬁned as a binding material that sets and hardens when reacting with water and is
capable of reacting under water [1]. The reactivity of the cement is among others governed by
the particle size and the chemical composition [25]. The pore solution in concrete is alkaline
containing dissolved ions. The ions mainly includes K+, Na+, Ca+ and OH−, which ensures a
pH of approximately 13 [22].
Portland cement was invented in 1824 by Joseph Aspdin. It is produced in a process where
limestone, sand, and clays are heated in a rotary kiln creating the material, clinker. The clinker
is ground into ﬁne cement particles.
The particles consist of diﬀerent clinker minerals, the
primary being C3S, C2S, C3A, and C4AF, see Tab. 2.1. The reaction between the minerals and
water is what causes the concrete to set and harden. [23]
Table 2.1: Chemical notations used to describe clinker minerals and oxides. [25]

CHAPTER 2. THEORY
2.1.1
Hydration
When the reaction between cement and water begins the paste start to harden. The hardened
paste is composed of the solid reaction products and voids, which are penetrable by water [18].
Many concrete properties can be attributed to the hydration of the cement where hydrates are
formed [28]. The products of the hydration is generally refereed to as the cement gel due to the
large speciﬁc internal surface and the total volume of space between the particles are refereed
to as gel porosity. [29, 18]
The four major hydrates of the cement is C3S, C2S, C3A, and C4AF. Since Portland cement
consist of more than one compound, the hydration process consist of several simultaneously
occurring reactions, see Eq. 2.1 to 2.6. However, the reaction of the aluminates are known to
be signiﬁcantly faster than the reaction of silicates. [25]
Usually, up to about 75% of the clinker minerals are silicates [25]. The clinker mineral, C3S,
contributes to the devolpment of both early and late strength development, whereas C2S manly
contributes to the late strength [23]. The chemical composition of the calcium silicate hydrates
is depended on the temperature, w/c-ratio, and degree of hydration. Therefore the hydrate is
normally refereed to as C-S-H. Assuming a complete hydration of the silicates, the reactions can
be written as Eq. 2.1 and 2.2 [25].
2C3S + 6H −→C3S3H3 + 3CH
(2.1)
2C2S + 4H −→C3S3H3 + CH
(2.2)
The clinker mineral C3A contributes to the early strength whereas C4AF does not contribute
signiﬁcantly to either the early or the late strength development.
[23] The hydration of the
aluminates takes place in two steps due to the presence of gypsum. Gypsum is added to the
cement to control the reaction of C3A, which would otherwise harden almost instantaneously
after mixing with water. The reation of C3A is desribed in Eq. 2.3 and 2.4 and the reaction of
C4AF is desribed in Eq. 2.5 and 2.6 [25].
Step 1:
C3A + 3CS∗H2 + 26H −→C3A · 3CS∗· H32
(2.3)
Step 2:
2C3A + C3A · 3CS∗· H32 + 4H −→3(C3A · CS∗· H12)
(2.4)
Step 1:
C4AF + 3CS∗H2 + 30H −→C3A · 3CS∗· H32 + CH + FH3
(2.5)
Step 2:
2C4AF + C3A · 3CS∗· H32 + 12H −→3(C3A · CS∗· H12) + 2CH + 2FH3
(2.6)

==PAGE:13==
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
The Degree of Hydration
To which extent the cement has hydrated can be expressed by the degree of hydration, α. The
degree of hydration can be calculated by Eq. 2.7 or Eq. 2.8 [18].
α =
mass of hydrated cement
mass of cement original in the mix
(2.7)
or
α =
mass of chemical bound water
maximum possible amount of chemical bound water
(2.8)
The maximum possible degree of hydration is depended on the w/c-ratio as a low w/c may
prevent full hydration.
If no water is available from an external source, then αmax < 1 for
w/c-ratio below 0.42. The time needed to reach the ultimate hydration furthermore increases
for decreasing w/c-ratios. However, full hydration might never be reach despite suﬃcient water
being available [18].
When considering the degree of hydration, some general deﬁnitions are often used. [28, 18] The
water can be divided into three diﬀerent types, the chemically bound water, the absorbed water
and the free water. However, the the diﬀerence between the three is not completely absolute
[18]. The chemical bound water, also referred to as the non-evaporable water, is a part of the
solid phases of the paste, which is bound during the hydration described by Eq. 2.1 to 2.6. It
evaporates at temperatures above 105 oC, when the phases of the paste decompose [28]. The
adsorbed water is physically adsorbed on the surface of the gel solid and occupies the gel pores.
The free water is the water, which occupies the capillary voids of the paste. The adsorbed water
together with the the free water is referred to as evaporable water that roughly corresponds to
the amount of water, which will evaporate when a paste sample is oven-dried at 105 oC. From
Power’s model, the volumetric composition of the hardened cement paste can be determined.
For every 1 g of hydrated cement, 0.19 g of water is adsorbed on the particles and 0.23 g are
chemically bound [18].
2.2
Fly Ash
Supplementary cementitious materials are used in concrete to improve its properties and to
optimize the environmental proﬁle.
Mineral additions which can work as a binder if mixed
with cement and reacts with calcium hydroxide is usually referred to as pozzolans [26].
Fly ash, slag and silica fume are pozzolanic materials, which according to ASTM C 125 is
deﬁned by its low or non existing cementitious value. Pozzolans will however react with calcium
hydroxide in the presence of moisture, creating compounds possessing cementitious properties [2].
The type ﬂy ash that is covered by the standard DS/EN 450-1:2012 is a byproduct from coal-ﬁred
power plants. It is formed at temperatures between 1200oC and 1600oC, where the nonﬂammable
elements melt together into small particles [6]. The particles mainly consist of SiO2, Al2O3, and
Fe2O3. [16] From micrographic analysis, it has been found that the majority of the particles are
solid spheres of glass. A small part of the particles may be hollow spheres, called cenospheres
(completely hollow) or plerospheres (packed with numerous small spheres). The particle size for
a typical ﬂy ash vary from <1 µm to about 100 µm [25].
5
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:14==
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
The product of ﬂy ash hydration is suggested to be more dense with a more gel-like structure
giving a ﬁner pore structure than the pure cement hydration [16].
Due to the necessity of
calcium hydroxide for the ﬂy ash reaction to take place, the reaction will not start immediately
after casting. The quality of the ﬂy ash determines how fast the ﬂy ash starts to react [6]. The
reaction can continue as long as calcium hydroxide is available in the pore ﬂuid and can be
written as Eq. 2.9 [25].
2S + 3CH −→C3S2H3
(2.9)
2.3
Durability of Concrete
The durability of concrete is, according to ACI Concrete Terminology, deﬁned as the ability
to resist the surrounding environment i.e.
chemical attack, abrasion, weathering, and other
conditions of service [1].
Well designed and well executed concrete can be durable in most
environments. However, premature deterioration do happen [25]. Knowledge about deterioration
mechanisms are essential to ensure that a structure can last throughout its service life. [29] But
as time passes, all materials will degrade as a results of changes in the microstructure and the
properties from environmental interactions [25]. In this section the four damage mechanisms salt
frost scaling, leaching, carbonation and bicarbonation will de explained.
2.3.1
Salt Frost Scaling
De-icing salts are regularly used during winters in areas of cold climate to ensure safety for the
users of roads and walkways. The salt is known to cause scaling of concrete structures exposed
to freezing which is a major durability problem [35].
The salt frost scaling is a superﬁcial
damage consisting of removal of small ﬂakes of material. Unlike interior frost damange, salt frost
scaling does not immediately aﬀect the safety or the mechanical integrity. However, it makes the
concrete susceptible to harmful ions and moisture transport from the surrounding environment
as the permeability is compromised by the scaling [35, 22]. The most detrimental concentration
of the freezing solution has been found to be 2-4 %. Experimental work shows this to be true
for diﬀerent kinds of freezing solutions, see Fig. 2.1 [37].
Figure 2.1: Eﬀect of concentration of freezing medium on the scaling. [37]
Katja Udbye Christensen
6
==PAGE:15==
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
In order to avoid durability problems caused by salt scaling, many countries have developed
testing methods to estimate the frost resistance of concrete before using a mix for a structure.
The methods usually include concrete specimens exposed to a solution containing a de-icing
salt which is then repeatedly frozen and thawed. The purpose of the tests are to simulate the
scaling and determine the resistance. However, as these tests are accelerated it has often been
discussed weather or not they truly are suitable for the purpose. [22]
There are several theories of which mechanism may cause the salt frost scaling but no agreement
has yet been reach [35].
Current theories include hydraulic pressure, glue spalling, and
crystallisation pressure among others [36].
The literature oﬀers both negative and positive observations of the frost resistance for concrete
with ﬂy ash [16]. According to [34] the durability can be compromised with regards to salt frost
scaling for high contents of ﬂy ash.
To obtain the best performance for concrete containing
ﬂy ash, an optimum of the ﬂy ash content exist for which the best combination of durability
and economy exist. The optimum amount will vary from project to project among others due
to diﬀerence in enviroment and the performance of ﬂy ash being dependent on the physical,
mineralogical and chemical properties. Observations show that scaling results tend to variate
more for ﬂy ash concrete due to its performance being more sensible to mixture proportioning,
method of placement, ﬁnishing, and curing. Furthermore, experiments indicate that laboratory
scaling tests does not necessarily corresponds to the ﬁeld performance of the concrete as the
results from laboratories seem more conservative [34]. Poor frost resistance of concrete with ﬂy
ash has also been attributed to a weaker surface than traditional concrete. In general a suﬃcient
frost resistance can be expected if the compressive strength at test initiation is minimum 40 MPa
[35].
Air Requirement
For concrete to be frost resistant, a system of ﬁnely distributed air voids is necessary.
Air
entraining agent can be added to stabilise the air bubbles that are created during mixing to
ensure suiﬃcient air content [16, 22].
Concrete which is not air entrained will only contain
natural air, entrapped during mixing. The natural air voids are generally larger than 1 mm,
which is not considered to contribute to the frost resistance.
For air entraining agent to
be eﬀective, the created air voids must be envenly distributed througout the concrete with a
diameter between 10µm and 300µm. An air content of 4-6% is normally desired for frost resistant
concretes. However, an increase in air causes a decrease in the strength. A rule of thumb is that
for each 1% of air the strength is decreased by 3-4%. The eﬀect of the agent can be aﬀected
by other constituents.
Cement particles can absorb the agent forming calcium salts on the
cement surface. Ions in the pore ﬂuid may neutralize the hydrophilic groups [41]. Furthermore,
unburned coal from the ﬂy ash can uptake and inactivate the air entraining agent thus reduce
the eﬀect of the agent. Fly ash can therefore make it troublesome to obtain a satifactory air void
system if the content of unburned carbon is too high and the dosage of AEA is not adjusted [16, 6].
The speciﬁc surface and the void spacing factor are often used to estimate whether or not a
concrete is frost resistant. The speciﬁc surface is deﬁned as the surface area of the air voids
relative to the volume. It increases when the size of the air bubbles decreases, thus the speciﬁc
surface is a measurement of the size of the voids [27, 22]. The void spacing factor is deﬁned as
the longest possible distance from a point in the paste to the nearest air void assuming equal
size air voids and distribution, see Fig. 2.2. According to Powers, the spacing factor should not
exceed 250 µm for frost resistant concrete [27]. However, some studies indicate that the critical
7
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:16==
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
spacing factor variates depending on the type of concrete. Evidence also point to the mass loss
being proportional to the spacing factor when the critcal value is exceeded. [35]
Figure 2.2: The assumptions made for the void spacing factor and the speciﬁc surface. [22]
The speciﬁc surface can be determined from the Eq. 2.10.

across intersected voids along the line of transverse, and A is the volume of voids in the concrete

air and the paste content of the concrete. If the speciﬁc surface increases, the air requirement
will decrease [27]. Although experiments show an improvement in the frost resistance for air
entrained concrete, some experiments indicate that for certain servere conditions entrained air
may not be suﬃcient protection. [37]
Freezing Point Depression
Freezing of a solution will occur when the chemical potential of the solution is equal to the
chemical potential of the solid. When dissolving a substance in water the amount of ions in
the solution increases, and this lower the chemical potential of the solution. When the chemical
potential decreases, the tendency to freeze is opposed, see Fig. 2.3. [5]
Katja Udbye Christensen

T ′ =

Where T is the freezing temperature of pure water, x is the molar fraction in the solution,
∆Hfreeze is the freezing enthalpy of pure water, R is the gas constant, and T ′ is the freezing
temperature of the solution. [5]
2.3.2
Leaching
The leaching process in concrete involves the transport of ions through the pore system out of
the concrete. During the process solid compounds are dissolved by water and transported away
[14]. Leaching is known to be a concern for concrete structures as calcium is removed from the
hydrates, leaving the surface more porous and susceptible to other kinds of deterioration. The
leaching mechanism is diﬀusion controlled but as the surface properties change with the degree
of leaching, the phenomena quickly become non-linear [17].
The hydration products and the pore ﬂuid is assumed to be in thermodynamic equilibrium.
If parameters such as temperature, pressure or composition of the pore ﬂuid is changed the
equilibrium is disturbed [14]. When the concrete is immersed in water, diﬀusion of ions out of
the material will begin induced by the chemical potential diﬀerence between the pore ﬂuid and
the external surface. As the the chemical composition in the pore ﬂuid changes, the equilibrium
in the system also changes, causing a dissulution of the calium hydroxide and later C-S-H. [17]
With the removal of CH and C-S-H from the paste, the fraction of solids decreases consequently
decreasing the strength [40].
The permeability of the concrete and the aggressiveness of the water is the most important
parameters controlling the extent of the leaching.
The pores of the concrete can either be
interconnected or non-interconnected. The water can only penetrate the concrete through the
interconnected pores, thus the pore size distribution and the connectivity are highly important
factors for the leaching resistance. Water with a pH lower than 12.5 is destabilising for the
hydration products and is therefore considered to be aggressive towards concrete. [14]
9
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:18==
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
The diﬀusion of ions and the permeability of water increases with increasing temperatures.
However, as the temperature increases the solubility of calcium hydroxide decreases. Thus the
eﬀect of the temperature on the rate of leaching is diﬃcult to estimate. The solubilty of calcium
hydroxide as a function of temeprature is written in Tab. 2.2 [14].
Table 2.2: The solubility of Ca(OH)2 [g CaO/L]. [14]
T [oC]

1.00
0.92
For concrete containing FA or other pozzolans the total content of CH is reduced as a result of
the hydration of FA with CH. A research project has shown an increase in leaching depth with
the content of ﬂy ash [40]. It is likely to be caused by an increased Ca+ conductivity of the pore
ﬂuid with a decrease in available calcium content. However, the research also showed a positive
eﬀect of ﬂy ash on the porosity and therefore also the strength compared to OPC concretes.[40]
2.3.3
Carbonation
During production of cement, limestone is burnt and CO2 gas is released in a process called
calcination. Theoretically, the hardened concrete will be able to bind the same amount of CO2
which has been released during burning. The binding process, refereed to as carbonation, is a
chemical reaction between carbon dioxide from the air and calcium hydroxide, calcium silicate
hydrates, and/or unhydrated cement particles [33]. One of the most important eﬀects of the
carbonation is a decrease of the pH of the pore solution, thus a decrease in the hydroxide
concentration [20].
Basically every environment contains CO2 and H2O, thus all concrete
structures will be exposed to carbonation, but the speed which a concrete carbonates depends
on the given environment and the concrete properties [21] .
The carbon dioxide from the air cannot react with the concrete hydrates when it is in a gas state.
Thus the carbon dioxide must be dissolved in water for the reaction process to be possible. The
process of carbonation is governed by the following reactions [33]:
CO2 + H2O ⇆HCO−
3 + H+
(2.14)
HCO−
3 ⇆CO2−
3
+ H+
(2.15)
Ca2+ + CO2−
3
⇆CaCO3
(2.16)
Ca(OH)2 ⇆Ca2+ + 2OH−
(2.17)
Ca2+ + CO2−
3
⇆CaCO3
(2.18)
The carbon dioxide in the system reacts with water from the pores to create hydrogen carbonate,
HCO−
3 .
The hydrogen carbonate splits in carbonate ions, CO−
3 and a hydrogen atom.
The
carbonate ions then react with calcium ions in the solution to precipitate calcium carbonate,
CaCO3.
As the calcium ions in the pore solution are consumed by the process, calcium
hydroxide or calcium silicate from the paste will dissolve and react. This process continuous
until no calcium is available. [33, 21]
Katja Udbye Christensen
10
==PAGE:19==
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
The product from the reactions are a low soluble calcium carbonate and silica gel formed from
the carbonation of calcium silicate hydrate. The calcium carbonate is found as small calcite
crystals which grows fast around the silica gel including it as impurities in the gel [33]. The
low solubility of the calcium carbonate will result in clogging of the pore system.
However,
microcracks are formed from the volume expansive reaction producing the calcium carbonate.
Thus it is diﬃcult to estimate if the carbonation increases or decreses the transportproperties
for gasses [20].
The carbonation process in a given environment depends on the water/cement-ratio which
controls the permeability and the relative amount of calcium components in the concrete.
The carbonation is a reaction starting at the surface and moving inwards.
In the case of
increased porosity near aggregates and cracks in the surface, the rate of carbonation will
increase [21]. When the calcium hydroxide near the surface is consumed by the carbonation,
the process will depend more and more on diﬀusion. As a result hereof, the carbonation process
becomes slower [20]. It is possible to calculated the carbonation rate, however, assumptions
and approximations of microclimate at the concrete surface and concrete quality must be made.
The majority of the carbonation occurs during the ﬁrst 50 years of exposure. [21]
The composition of the pore solution changes over time for concrete. It determines which of the
solid phases are stable and may precipitate and which phases are unstable and may dissolve.
For concrete containing SCM’s, the alkali content decreases over time as the reaction of the
SCM’s take place. This is related to the formation of C-S-H with a lower Ca/Si ratio where more
alkalis are bound. The eﬀect of the alkalis and hydroxide content in pore solution from adding
ﬂy ash varies depending on amount of alkalis present in the ﬂy ash. The decrease of the alkali
concentraion results in a reduction of the hydroxide content thus a reduction in the pH of the
pore solution [38]. According to [39], concrete with a low Ca/Si-relation decomposes faster as a
result of carbonation compared to a concrete with a high Ca/Si-relation [39].
2.3.4
Bicarbonation
Bicarbonation has been detected in several low quality concretes with high water/cement-
ratios. Unlike regular carbonation, bicarbonation reduces both the strength and increases the
permeability of the concrete surface. The calcite in the paste precipitates into large crystals
surrounded by a silica gel. The crystals do not have any binding eﬀect thus the new binder
becomes the decalciﬁed silica gel. [33]
Bicarbonation appears in concrete with a low content of calcium hydroxide. A normal pore solu-
tion has a pH value above 12 which is maintained by the presence of calcium hydroxide. As the
calcium hydroxide reacts and disappears from the concrete the pH value drops. As seen on Fig.
2.4, carbonate ions are most stable at high pH values. At pH below 11 the carbonate ion con-
centration decreases and the hydrogen carbonate concentration increases making it increasingly
diﬃcult to grow calcite crystals. [33]
11
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:20==
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
Figure 2.4: Stability of carbonate species depending on pH. [33]
Whether the carbonation in a concrete is regular carbonation or bicarbonation is determined by
the availability of calcium hydroxide and the concentration of carbon dioxide. Pozzolans may
aﬀect the bicarbonation both negatively and positively as the presence of pozzolans decreases
the permeability but also reduces the content of calcium hydroxide. [33]
2.3.5
Synergy
A concrete structure may be resistant to a certain degradation mechanism.
However, if the
concrete is subjected to several mechanisms this might reduce the resistance towards the ﬁrst
mechanism.
This is refereed to as synergy, where the interaction between elements together
produce a larger eﬀect than the sum of the eﬀects individually [29].
Concrete exposed to
seawater is especially vulnerable as several degradation mechanisms may occur at the same
time [25]. An example of synergy could be the combination of bicarbonation and leaching. The
presence of leaching makes concrete more susceptible to the formation of bicarbonation due
to the already low pH value of the pore ﬂuid. The presence of the bicarbonation then further
decreases the durability of the concrete surface [14].
Another example of synergy is presented by [30]. The cause of damage to concrete near the
waterline in hydraulic structures could be explained by a combination of leaching, abrasion, and
frost action. An analysis of concrete samples taken from the buttress dam, showed leaching of the
paste in two steps. The ﬁrst step includes dissolution of the calcium hydroxide, ettringite, and
calcium rich C-S-H causing a reduction in the porosity. In the second step, further dissolution
of C-S-H occurs. The content of CaO, SiO2 and the total oxide content is given in Fig. 2.5. The
concrete that is about to undergo step two or has already undergone the step is more susceptible
to freezing in the presence of deionised water. [30]
Figure 2.5: Content of CaO in the paste as a function of the distance from the surface. [30]
Katja Udbye Christensen

==PAGE:21==

Materials and Method
This chapter contains information about the materials used and the diﬀerent tests that have
been performed. The tests includes casting, quality checks of the properties, freeze/thaw tests,
and tests used to evaluate the conditions of the exposed specimens.
3.1
Test Plan
During the pre-project conducted in the spring of 2016, ﬁve concrete mixes with increasing
content of ﬂy ash was developed.
The goal was to create ﬁve concretes with comparable
properties, namely the slump, the air content, and the compressive strength. The developed
concretes had a ﬂy ash/cement ratio of 0.00, 0.33, 0.50, 0.80, and 1.00 by mass. The mixes were
named 000, 033, 050, 080, and 100 respectively.
The test plan for this project is divided into two parts. Each part deals with one of the two
hypotheses described in the introduction. Three series of concrete are cast including recasting
of mix 080 and 100, plus developing and casting of a new mix called 100 EA. 100 EA is identical
to mix 100 expect for the content of chemical admixtures as the content of air entraining agent
is increased.
The following tests will be performed to investigate the eﬀect of hollow ﬂy ash grains on the air
structure analysis:
• Freeze/thaw tests conducted on concrete with extra air entrainment to see if the presence
of extra air reduces the extent of the scaling.
• Air void analysis on paste samples without air entraining agent to estimate if a higher
content of ﬂy ash leads to more registered air voids.
The following tests will be performed to investigate the possible eﬀect of leaching:
• Termogravimetric analyses conducted on crushed paste samples. From the analysis it can
be found how much of the cement and ﬂy ash has reacted forming a basis for determining
the maximum recommend ﬂy ash content if leaching is the damage mechanism.
• Diﬀerent variations of freeze/thaw testing to see if scaling can either be avoided by reducing
the leaching rate or increased by accelerating the rate of leaching.
• Microscopic analysis of the samples prior to and after exposure to freeze/thaw. The anal-
yses can be used to evaluate the conditions of the samples and to investigate if leaching
has occurred.

==PAGE:22==
CHAPTER 3. MATERIALS AND METHOD
In addition to the test mentioned above, the properties such as slump, air content, air void
analysis and strength are measured as a quality check for each cast concrete mix. The target
slump of the fresh concrete is 125 mm +/- 15 mm, the target air content of the fresh concrete
is 6% +/- 1%, the maximum allowed spacing factor is according to [8] equal to 0.2 mm and the
target strength after 28 days of hardening is 42 MPa.

3.3
Methods
3.3.1
Trial Mixes
The recipe of 080 and 100 developed in the pre-project was reused, see Tab. 3.3 for details. A
new mix, 100 EA, was developed with extra air entraining agent. A trial mix was mixed on
the machine shown in Fig. 3.1. The recipe was developed with a method where the content of
aggregates stay constant, thus only the content of water, cement, ﬂy ash, and chemical admixtures
are changed. Previous experience from DTI has shown the given method leads to comparable
strengths for a series of recipes. Since the necessary content of air entraining agent normally
increases with the content of ﬂy ash, which was not the case for the previous developed recipes
see Tab. 3.3, it was decided to add the same amount of AEA as added in mix 033. On the trial
mix, the slump was measured and the content of super plasticiser was adjusted until the desired
properties was reached.
Figure 3.1: Mixer used for trial mixes.

laboratory on two separate dates. The ﬁrst day, the samples used for air void analysis are cast
and the second day, the specimens used for TGA are cast. The procedure is the same for the
two batches.

3.3.8
TGA
Termogravimetric analysis, TGA, is a measurement technique which can be used to estimate the
degree of hydration and the reactivity of ﬂy ash or other SCM’s. When a sample is exposed
to temperatures high enough, the minerals and hydrates will decompose resulting in a change
in mass as water is released. The temperature at which this occurs is speciﬁc for the given
mineral or hydrate. By increasing the temperature while measuring the mass it becomes possible
to determine the content of some hydrates and minerals.
Calcium hydroxide decomposes at
temperatures around 460oC, see Fig 3.7, whereas C-S-H decomposes at a temperature range of
50 to 600oC and CaCO3 at between 600 to 800 oC. [31]
Figure 3.7: Weight loss (solid line) and diﬀerential thermogravimetry (dotted line) from decom-
posing of calcium hydroxide. [31]
The results obtained from the TGA, is inﬂuenced by various parameters such as heating rate,
gas ﬂow rate, pretreatment of the sample, the type of vessel used, and the sample size. Thus
the results will change if a diﬀerent testing procedure is used. Due to large uncertainties in the
measurements, the sample size should not be less than 50 mg. [31]
TGA measurements are conducted on paste specimens after 1 day, 3, days, 7 days, and 28 days of
hydration. The samples are crushed into ﬁne powder and approximately 5 g are placed in a glass
container, see Fig. 3.8. To ensure that no further hydration occurs, the hydration is stopped by
placing the samples in vacuum. After 1 hour of vacuum, the samples are placed in an oven at
105oC. During vacuum and heating in the oven, the physically bound water and the capillary
water will evaporate from the samples so only the chemically bound water is left. When the
samples have been in the oven for 24 hours they are removed and placed in an desiccator with
silica gel in the bottom. The silica gel absorb moisture from the air, ensuring that hydration can
not restart.
Katja Udbye Christensen
20
==PAGE:29==
CHAPTER 3. MATERIALS AND METHOD
Figure 3.8: Crushed paste samples used for TGA.
The TGA is carried out using a Jupiter STA 449 F3 machine as shown on Fig. 3.9 with platinum
crucibles. A sample size between 50-60 mg is placed in the crucibles. When the sample is placed,
the analysis is initiated. The temperature is increased with 10 K pr. minute until 1050 oC is
reach while the weight is constantly measured. The results obtained are corrected for loss of
ignition of the ﬂy ash and the cement.
Figure 3.9: Machine used for TGA.
21
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:30==
CHAPTER 3. MATERIALS AND METHOD
3.3.9
Freeze/Thaw Test
The freeze/thaw tests are conducted at the laboratory at DTI. Four diﬀerent tests courses are
carried out:
• Freeze/thaw according to DS/CEN/TS 12390-9:2006. The freeze/thaw tests are
conducted in accordance with DS/CEN/EN 12390-9:2006 on ﬁve specimens and serves as
a reference to the other tests.
• Freeze/thaw with increased pH of freezing solution.
The freeze/thaw tests are
conducted with an increased pH of the freezing solution. The purpose is to investigate
if it decreases the scaling which would indicate that leaching could be the cause of the
durability issues. The tests are initiated with a solution consisting of 3 % NaCl and 0.1 %
NaOH. The solution has a pH of approximately 13 and freezing point of 270.74 K which is
0.47 K lower than the regular 3 % NaCl solution.
• Freeze/thaw with CaCl2. The freeze/thaw tests are conducted with a calcium chloride
solution with a freezing point equivalent to a 3 % NaCl solution. The theory is that calcium
chloride might be less aggressive towards the concrete with respect to leaching as the ions
are more like the ions of the pore solution.
• Freeze/thaw with replacement of the freezing solution. The freeze/thaw tests are
conducted with a 3 % NaCl solution, which is changed 5 days during a week. The theory is
that replacement of the solution will increase the leaching and thus increasing the scaling
if leaching is the damaging mechanism.
3.3.10
Petrographic Analysis
The petrographic analysis is performed in accordance with ASTM C856. The analysis is per-
formed on a ﬂuorescent impregnated thin section with an area of 30 x 45 mm.
3.3.11
Scanning Electron Microscopy
The SEM-EDX analysis is performed on polished sections using a Qunata 400 from FEI. An
accelerating voltage 20 KeV, spot size 5 and a working distance of around 10 mm are used
during analysis. The analysis is done on coal-coated samples in low vacuum mode. The data is
corrected for the absorption, the ﬂorescence, and the atom number.
Katja Udbye Christensen
22

Results and Discussion
This chapter is divided in two parts, each part deals with one of the hypotheses. The results
also include data from the pre-project. To make it possible to distinguish, all results from the
pre-project will be marked with a p.
4.1
Hypothesis 1
The air void structure is important for the frost resistance of the concrete. It is essential that the
measurements are reliable as these measurements are often used to verify the frost resistance.
This sections consist of an air void analyses performed on paste samples and on concrete samples.

According to the results, ﬂy ash in large quantities alone can ensure a satisfying spacing factor.
Paste samples 050, 080 and 100 all appear to meet the requirements whereas 000 and 033 do not.
However, the obtained spacing factors create an artiﬁcial images of the air void distribution for
the samples, as the low spacing factors are not likely to be a result of the presence of actual air
voids but rather the presence of cenospheres from the ﬂy ash. In Fig. 4.3 a hollow ﬂy ash grain
can be seen in ﬂorescent light.
Figure 4.3: Thin section of 080p in ﬂourenscent light, the red arrow marks a cenosphere and the
blue bar corresponds to 200 µm.
The grain in the picture is approximately 70 µm wide and would therefore be registered as an air
void in the analyses. The hollow ﬂy ash grain does not have the same positive eﬀect on the frost
resistance as air voids, since the cavity is closed of from the rest of the system. It is therefore
relevant to see how much the presence of ﬂy ash would aﬀect an analysis performed on a concrete
sample. If the consequence of the cenospheres are so signiﬁcant that concrete with insuﬃcient
air void systems are passed as acceptable it can explain why some structures shows unexpected
frost damage.
Katja Udbye Christensen
24
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
The cumulated no. of air voids measured on the paste specimens are shown in Fig. 4.4, the left
being the full spectrum of the analyses and the right being for the range of 0-160 µm.
Figure 4.4: Cumulated no. of voids
No air entraining agent was added to the paste mix thus the voids created during mixing are
unstable and will often disappear. Therefore only a minimum content of small voids would be
expected. However, as it can been seen from the results in Tab. 4.1 and in Fig. 4.4 this is not the
case for concrete containing ﬂy ash. The typical particle size for ﬂy ash grains are between <1
µm to about 100 µm. Thus if the ﬂy ash grains are falsely registered as air voids, they would not
aﬀect the large chord sizes. According to Fig. 4.4 the total number of registered chords increases
with the content of ﬂy ash up to a chord size of approximately 120 µm.
Therefore further
data processing concerns the chord sizes between 0-120 µm. To further support this theory, the
registered number of chords as a function of the ﬂy ash content are plotted in Fig. 4.5 for chord
size 120-140 µm. If cenospheres are registered as air voids, the number of registrations must
increase linearly with the content of ﬂy ash. From the ﬁgure, it is clear that there does not exist
any connection for the plotted chord size.
Figure 4.5: The no. of chords for the chord size 120-140 µm as as function of the volume of FA.
25
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:34==
CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
The number of registered air voids are plotted as a function of the volume of ﬂy ash in Fig. 4.6
for each measured chord interval between 0-120 µm.
Figure 4.6: The no. of chords for each chords size as a function of the volume of FA.
A linear relation is estimated for each chord size. The relation supports the theory that the
registered air voids in reality are cenospheres. The inclination of the lines give an estimate of
how much the air void analysis is aﬀected by the hollow spheres in the ﬂy ash. The larger the
slope of the linear relation the more chords are falsely measured. The inclinations found in the
analyses from Fig. 4.6 are summarized in Fig. 4.7.
Figure 4.7: The slopes for each chord size.

The largest eﬀect on the air void analyses is found for chord size 10-20 µm after which the eﬀect
decreases. The only analysed chord size which seems to deviate from the general trend is chord
size 0-10 µm. This is likely due to the chords in the interval between 0-6 µm being ﬁltered out
as they are often irregularities in the samples or small defects.
The results can be used to re-evaluate the measured air void structure of the concrete as it gives
an estimate to how much the analyses overrate the number of chords for the diﬀerent chord
sizes and diﬀerent contents of ﬂy ash.

4.2
Hypothesis 2
When degradation mechanisms aﬀect the concrete the properties of the concrete changes and the
durability is compromised. If concrete containing ﬂy ash is more susceptible to bicarbonation
and/or leaching it is vital to clarify as it can aﬀect the service life of the structures. The section
will contain the fresh concrete properties, TGA results performed on paste samples, freeze/thaw
tests, and microscopic analyses.
4.2.1
Fresh Concrete Properties
The measurements on the fresh concrete is used as a quality check of the concrete prior to casting.
The air content gives an estimate of whether or not the content of AEA is suﬃcient to ensure
the necessary frost resistance and the slump is measured to ensure a satisfying workability.
The measured slump of the fresh concrete is summarised in Tab. 4.4.

chemical admixtures.

4.2.2
Compressive Strength
The compressive strength is an important parameter for the frost resistance. According to [34],
concrete containing ﬂy ash can be considered frost resistant if the compressive strength is larger
than 40 MPa and the air void structure is satisfying. The compressive strengths measured are
summarized in Tab. 4.6 in addition with the expected compressive strength that are based on
the measurements conducted on the trial mixes.

CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
The lowest measured strength is for mix 033p and the highest is for 100p. The concrete with
high contents of ﬂy ash all obtain high strengths. It can therefore be excluded that a low early
strength due to the slow reaction of ﬂy ash is the cause of the extensive scaling.
Originally it was believed that the high strength of 080p and 100p was a result of a casting error
as extra AEA and SP was necessary to reach the target values for air content and slump. The
need for extra super plasticiser could indicate an incorrect low water content which would cause
an increased strength. However, it was not necessary to add extra chemical admixtures to mix
080 and 100 and they still obtained higher strengths than expected. It could also have been an
indication of a low air content as 1% of air reduces the compressive strength 3-4%. But as the air
void analyses revealed satisfying air contents, this is neither a likely explanation. Nevertheless,
a higher strength should make the concrete less susceptible to frost action.
4.2.3
TGA
The TGA tests are used to evaluate the quality of the concrete prior to exposure. The TGA
gives information about the change of mass during heating, which can be used to estimate the
degree of hydration, the content of Ca(OH)2, and the reactivity of the ﬂy ash.
The mass loss during heating is partly due to the loss on ignition of the binder used for casting.
The change of mass for the ﬂy ash and cement are shown in Fig. 4.8. The mass change is
measured from 105oC and up to 1050oC which corresponds to the temperature range the paste
samples are measured in.
Figure 4.8: Loss on ignition of the cement and ﬂy ash.
The total mass loss for the cement was measured to 1.92 % and the total mass loss for the
ﬂy ash was measured to 2.39 %. The expected mass loss for the paste samples are calculated
to between 1.32 to 1.45 % of the total mass. A decrease in mass can have an essential eﬀect
of the calculated degree of hydration. The plot in Fig. 4.8 shows that the loss on ignition is
not rapidly increasing at 460oC where the calcium hydroxide decomposes.
Thus the loss on
ignition is not expected to have any signiﬁcant eﬀect of calculation of the content of calcium hy-
droxide. Therefore the calculations of calcium hydroxide will not be corrected for loss on ignition.
31
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:40==
CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
The measured mass losses after 1, 3, 7, and 28 days after casting are summarised in Fig. 4.9. The
mass loses are not corrected for the correction measurements performed with empty crucibles
as the main disturbance occurs before 105 oC and therefore not aﬀects the results. The other
variations to the mass measurements are minimal and it is evaluated that these disturbances can
be neglected.
1 day
3 days
7 days
28 days
Figure 4.9: Mass change of paste samples during heating.
The large drop in mass at around 460oC can be attributed to the release of water when calcium
hydroxide decomposes. Paste sample 000 generally shows the largest mass loss and 100 shows
the smallest. This is expected as 000 contains more cement and will therefore also bind more
water. The tangential method is used estimate the change in mass, where a linear relation is
estimated on each side of the drop, see Fig. 4.10. The temperature where the heat release is
the highest is used to calculate an upper and lower value, the diﬀerence between the two values
corresponds to the mass of water released. The method is further described in [31].
Katja Udbye Christensen
32
==PAGE:41==

Figure 4.10: Example of the tangential method used for calculations of the calcium hydroxide
content for sample 100 after 28 days.
From the calculated mass loss at around 460 oC, the calcium hydroxide content is calculated by
using Eq. 4.2. The Ca(OH)2/cement ratio is afterwards calculated by using Eq. 4.3. The ratio
shows how much calcium hydroxide is formed relative to the cement content.
The results are summarized in Fig. 4.11.
mCa(OH)2 = mH2O · nCa(OH)2
nH2O
(4.2)
mCa(OH)2
mcement
=
mCa(OH)2
mafter · (1 −LOI) · mc
(4.3)
Where mafter is the mass after heating to a 1050 oC, LOI is the loss on ignition, and mc is the
cement mass fraction of the total binder content.
Figure 4.11: Content of Ca(OH)2 in the paste samples.
The content of calcium hydroxide starts to decrease somewhere between 7 to 28 days after
casting for all paste samples containing ﬂy ash. The decrease indicates that the ﬂy ash has
started to react and the developed calcium hydroxide is transformed into C-S-H. The rate of
the cement hydration can be estimated from the Ca(OH)2 /cement ratio. All samples follow the
approximately same rate for the ﬁrst 7 days of hardening. At initiation of the freeze/thaw tests
(28 days after casting) there are still calcium hydroxide present in all the samples. Theoretically,
only 0.3 g of ﬂy ash will be able to react if 1 g of cement is fully hydrated, see calculations in
33
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:42==
CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Appendix B. Thus all paste samples containing ﬂy ash would contain enough ﬂy ash to use up
all the calcium hydroxide. After 28 days, paste sample 000 contains 14.9 % calcium hydroxide
which corresponds to a degree of hydration of 0.59.
The degree of hydration based on the total mass loss are calculated from Eq. 4.4 and Eq. 4.5
where Eq. 4.4 are based on the total content of binder and Eq. 4.5 is based only on the cement
content.
αb = mbefore −mafter/(1 −LOI)
0.23 · mafter/(1 −LOI)
(4.4)
αc = mbefore −mafter · mc/(1 −LOI)
0.23 · mafter · mc/(1 −LOI)
(4.5)
Where mbefore is the mass after heating to a 105 oC, mafter is the mass after heating to a 1050
oC, LOI is the loss on ignition, mc is the cement mass fraction of the total binder content.
The calculated results are summarised in Fig. 4.12. The calculations are both based on total
binder content and the cement content.
Degree of hydration calculated for the total binder con-
tent.
Degree of hydration calculated for the cement content.
Figure 4.12: The calculated degree of hydration.
Originally in the calculations for the degree of hydration, the maximum possible content of
chemical bound water is calculated as the cement mass times 0.23. When using this approach,
the degree of hydration of all samples containing ﬂy ash exceeds 1. A degree of hydration larger
than 1 (100 %) does not have any physical meaning. From Eq. 2.9 the puzzolanic reaction is
summarised. It is assumed that SiO2 is the only part of the ﬂy ash that react. However, other
chemical compounds in the ﬂy ash may also react and bind water which is then released during
heating, aﬀecting the mass loss. The issues with the total mass loss might also be attributed to
the measurements. According to [31], the mass loss of ﬂy ash during heating can be attributed
to the burning of left over coal. But as nitrogen is injected into the machine during testing, it
should not be possible for a combustion to take place.
Katja Udbye Christensen
34
==PAGE:43==
CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
The degree of hydration of 000 is plotted in Fig. 4.13 based on the calcium hydroxide content
and on the total mass loss.
Figure 4.13: The degree of hydration of 000 calculated from to the total mass loss and from the
Ca(OH)2.
After 28 of hardening, the degree of hydration for 000 is measured to 0.93 based on the total
mass loss and to 0.59 based on the calculated Ca(OH)2 content which is diﬀerence of a factor
0.6. The degree of hydrations measurements from [32] showed a calcium hydroxide content after
28 days of approximately 15 % for paste sample made of Ordinary Portland cement with a w/c
ratio of 0.45. The value is comparable to the one found for 000, however Rapid Portland cement
would be expected to have a faster reaction and therefore also a higher content of Ca(OH)2.
Results from [19] gave a degree of hydration of 0.7 for a paste sample made of Rapid Portland
cement and with a w/c ratio of 0.45. This value is between the two others and does therefore
not give an indication of which value is more reliable. It might have been possible to evaluate
the accuracy of the degree of hydration by heating crushed paste samples in an oven at 1050 o
C and measuring the mass loss in that way. However, due to times pressure, this was not possible.
4.2.4
Freeze/Thaw Tests
As concrete structures in colder climates are often exposed to freezing it is important for the
durability that the concrete can withstand the damaging mechanism. During the pre-project, it
was concluded that the damage to concrete with high contents of ﬂy ash could not be attributed
to a chemical reaction between the concrete and NaCl as high amounts of scaling also occurred
when using urea as freezing medium. For this thesis, four diﬀerent variations of the freeze/thaw
test are conducted. The purpose is to try either to decrease or increase the scaling in order to
demonstrate the presence of bicarbonation or leaching.
35
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:44==
CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
All the scaling results from the tests conducted according to the standard DS/CEN/TS 12390-
9:2006 are illustrated in Fig. 4.14 and serves as a reference to the other freeze/thaw tests.
Figure 4.14: The scaling after freeze/thaw testing according to DS/CEN/TS 12390-9:2006.
Results from the pre-project showed an increase in scaling with the increase of the ﬂy ash
content. However, the mass loss for 100EA has proven to be signiﬁcantly lower than the mass
loss of mix 080 and 100. Since the only diﬀerence between the mixes are the content of AEA
and therefore also the air void system it can not be excluded that the air in the concretes plays
an important factor. According to [35], the critical spacing factor may vary for diﬀerent types of
concrete and the amount of scaling is indicated to be proportional with the extend to which the
spacing factor exceed the critical value. So despite all specimens obtaining a satisfying spacing
factor according to standards, the spacing factors may not be suﬃcient for the concrete type.
In Fig. 4.15 the scaling after 42 cycles of freeze/thaw with 3% NaCl is plotted as a function of
the adjusted spacing factor. Due to issues with the frost cabinet the tests was delayed and it
was not possible to get the scaling after 56 cycles for all specimens.
Figure 4.15: The scaling after 42 cycles with 3% NaCl as a function of the spacing factor.
There appear to be a general trend, where the concrete with the highest spacing factors also
shows the highest level of scaling. The results indicates that the issues with scaling could be
Katja Udbye Christensen
36
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
solved by lowering the spacing factor. However, this does not explain why the critical spacing
factor appear to be lower for ﬂy ash concrete. During the last cycles of 080p, 050p, and 100 the
frost cabinet broke down and temperatures reached 40 degrees. The subsequent measurements
shows an increase in scaling compared to the previous obtained results. The rate of chemical
reactions are temperature dependent and increases with increasing temperatures, which could
indicate that the amount of scaling is also connected to a chemical damage mechanism, such
as leaching and bicarbonation. All frost results where there has been a break down of the frost
cabinet are marked by a dotted line.
Samples from mix 080 and mix 100 was exposed to NaOH solution to see if increasing the pH
of the solution could prevent leaching. A reduction in scaling compared to the reference would
indicate the presence of leaching. The results are plotted in Fig. 4.16 with the reference values.
Figure 4.16: The scaling after exposure to either a 3% NaCl or a 3% NaCl with NaOH.
According to [14] the solubility of calcium hydroxide is hardly aﬀected by a solution with a
concentration on 0.1 % NaOH. This was, however, discovered after the test initiation. It was
therefore decided to increase the concentration from 0.1 % to 0.5 % after 28 cycles of exposure.
Despite the increase in the concentration there is no signiﬁcant diﬀerence in the scaling between
the samples exposed to a pure NaCl solution and the samples exposed to a NaCl solution with
NaOH. The reason why there seem to be no eﬀect of the increase in pH can either be explained
by leaching not being a durability problem or that it is simply not enough to increase the pH to
slow down the leaching rate.
37
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:46==
CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Samples from mix 100EA was exposed to a 4% CaCl2 solution. Opposite NaOH, CaCl2 is more
similar to the pore solution of the concrete and could therefore be considered less hostile to the
concrete. The results are plotted in Fig. 4.17 with the reference values. The mass loss measured
after 56 cycles are not included due to delays after breakdowns of the frost cabinet.
Figure 4.17: The scaling after exposure to NaCl or CaCl2
The scaling for the samples exposed to CaCl2 and the NaCl obtain similar results until the
measurement after 14 cycles where the scaling accelerate for the specimens exposed to CaCl2.
According to the Fig. 2.1, a lower severity in damage can be expected for samples exposed to
CaCl2. It would therefore have been expected that the scaling was somewhat higher for the
specimens exposed to NaCl. The results can not be used to verify the presence of either leaching
or bicabonation.
The last freeze/thaw test was conducted to further investigate the possible eﬀect of leaching on
the scaling rate. Specimens from mix 100 and 100EA was exposed to a regular 3% NaCl solution
which was changed ﬁve times per week. The changing of the solution would be expected to
increase the leaching as the specimens will then constantly be subjected to a solution aggressive
towards the concrete. The measured results are shown in Fig. 4.18 together with the reference
values.
Figure 4.18: The scaling after regular exposure to NaCl or chancing of solution 5 days/week
Katja Udbye Christensen
38
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
The test was terminated after only 14 cycles.
The specimens from mix 100 had previously
undergone 56 cycles of freezing and thawing with NaCl but was reused for this test. Therefore
an increase in scaling could also be attributed to the already damaged surface.
The frost
cabinet furthermore broke down during the test, which from previous results has shown to
cause an increase in the scaling rate.
The result from testing on the specimens from mix
100 can therefore not be directly compared to previous obtained results.
Since there was
no diﬀerence in the measurements on mix 100EA it was decided not to continue testing.
In connection with the chancing of the solution, the pH value was measured.
The results
showed an increase in pH from approximately 8 to approximately 10.5 after 1 day of freeze and
thawing. The increase in pH indicated leaching whereas the scaling results indicates the opposite.
From all the diﬀerent versions of the freeze/thaw test conducted it can be concluded that the
scaling for concrete with high contents of ﬂy ash can be signiﬁcantly reduced by lowering the
spacing factor. The reason why a smaller spacing factor is so eﬀective could be related to the
quality of the surface. If the surface quality is reduced the concrete might require a ﬁner air void
distribution to be frost resistance. Whereas the increase in scaling at break down of the frost
cabinet and the increase of the pH of the freezing solution could indicate the presence of leaching
and bicarbonation, the scaling results from the varies test indicates otherwise.
4.2.5
Microscopic Analysis
The microscopic analyses is used to evaluate the conditions of the samples before and after
exposure. The analysis consists of a petrographic analysis performed on thin sections and a
SEM analysis performed on polished sections.
Petrographic Analysis
The thin sections are investigated in ﬂorescent, polarised- and x-polarised light. The diﬀerent
light settings can reveal diﬀerent aspects of the conditions of the concrete.
During the
pre-project, it was established that there was no signiﬁcant diﬀerence in the conditions of the
specimens prior to exposure to freeze/thaw testing. General for the thin sections was the present
of empty, surface parallel cracks to a depth of maximum 0.6 mm. The cracks development was
attributed to the saw used for cutting the specimens.
It was decided not to focus the petrographic analyses on the specimens exposed to NaOH as
there was no improvement with regards to the mass loss after exposure to freezing and thawing.
The analysis will therefore solely concern mix 080 and 100 exposed to NaCl. The thin sections
will be compared to 000p which serves as a reference. As the state of the specimens prior to
exposure has already been established, this part of the report will only deal with the conditions
of the specimens of 080 and 100 after 42 cycles of exposure.
39
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:48==
CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Petrographic images of 000p, 080, and 100 are shown in ﬂorescent light in Fig. 4.19 after exposure.
000p
080
100
Figure 4.19: Thin sections after exposure to NaCl in ﬂorescent light. Red arrows shows the
scaled surface and the blue bar corresponds to 1 mm.
The surface of 000p appears mostly intact with sporadic scaling to a maximum depth of 0.6
mm whereas the surface of 080 and 100 clearly shows scaling to a depth of 0.6 mm and 0.8 mm
respectively. According to the results summarized in Fig. 4.14, 080 and 100 have obtained a
comparable mass loss after 42 cycles in contrast to 000p, which shows a minimum amount of
scaling. This corresponds well with the ﬁndings in the petrographic analyses.
The air content of all thin sections is high.
However, the air distribution for 000p appears
unevenly. According to the adjusted air void analysis on the concrete specimens, the total air
content of 000p was 7.33 %, for 080 it was 3.14 % and for 100 it was 3.93 %.

Figure 4.21: Thin sections after exposure to NaCl in polarised- and x-polarised light. The red

Calcium hydroxide is visible in the entire thin section for 000p unlike 080 and 100 where the
calcium hydroxide is hardly visible.
According to the TGA results, the content of calcium
hydroxide in 000p should be around 2 times as high as for 080 and almost 3 times as high as
for 100 at test initiation. However, as this is not reﬂected in the petrographic images it could
indicate a decomposition of the calcium hydroxide during the exposure to freezing and thawing.
Yellow grains are present in 080 and 100. They are most likely Friedels’ salt. The surface of
080 and 100 appears to be aﬀected by bicarbonation behind the scaled edge down to a depth
of approximately 0.4 mm. The surface of 000p does not show signs of carbonation but a calcite
crust can sporadic be observed. Calcite crusts are also observed several places in the thin sections
of 080 and 100. Bicarbonation is known to create a porous paste which also corresponds well
to the ﬁndings in Fig. 4.20. The petrographic analysis is evidence of a change in the surface
conditions which appear after exposure as bicarbonation is formed. However, the analyses can
not give an estimate to at which extent the bicarbonation aﬀects the scaling results.
41
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:50==
CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Scanning Electron Microscopy
A SEM analysis can be used both to evaluate the conditions of the sample from images but can
also be used to conduct a chemical analyses of the paste composition. During the pre-project, an
analyses was performed on polished sections. The results showed the presence of bicarboation
for all samples containing ﬂy ash. It furthermore revealed crack development to a small extent
compared to the already developed cracks from sawing.
The chemical data extracted in the pre-project is re-evaluated in this report. From the chemical
analyses it is possible to extract the mole fraction of calcium, the result are summarised in Tab.
4.7. There was not conducted any chemical analysis of the surface for sample 033p prior to
exposure.

==PAGE:53==
CHAPTER 4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Prior to exposure the chemical composition of the bulk paste and the surface is somewhat the
same. After the exposure the bulk paste stay unchanged. However, the composition of the paste
near the surface changes for all concretes containing ﬂy ash. During bicarbonation the calcium
is dissolved and precipitates into large crystals. The binding part of the paste then becomes
the silica gel. If the silica gel is susceptible to other ions it is likely that it binds sodium from
the freezing solution.
The analyses proves that a change occur in the concrete with ﬂy ash
in the exposed surface, whereas the bulk paste stay unchanged. In connection with the small
crack development in the exposed samples, the chemical change of the surface might point to the
scaling being a phenomenon of poor surface quality which develops during testing.
4.2.6
Microscopic Investigations from the Field
The specimens used for the petrographic analysis and the SEM analysis was tested for 42 cycles
according to DS/CEN/TS 12390-9:2006. However, some criticise the test method for not being
comparable to the ﬁeld exposure. It is therefore relevant to see if leeching and bicarbonation
are observed in concrete structures exposed to frost temperatures.
Petrographic analyses have been conducted on the Farøe bridge by the Concrete Expert Centre
[7]. The concrete used for the bridge consist of low alkali cement with substitution of ﬂy ash.
The air content was estimated to 3-4 % and the w/c-ratio to 0.4. The actual content of ﬂy
ash is unknown. The analysis revealed a scaled surface, cracked paste, ettringite formation in
air voids and bicarbonation. It was furthermore established that the bulk paste contained a
high level of calcium hydroxide compared to the paste near the surface. Many of the ﬁndings
are similar to those found for 080 and 100, which may indicate that the phenomenon observed
occurs in marine environments.
A chemical analysis was performed on the Farøe bridge by the Expert Concrete Center. The
results are shown in Fig 4.24.
Figure 4.24: Chemical Analysis from the Farøe bridge [7] as a function o the distance from the
surface in mm.
The analysis shows a similar tendency as Fig. 2.5 with regards to the calcium content. There is
a large drop in the content around 4 mm from the surface, which indicated that only the outer
part of the surface is aﬀected by changes, likely from leaching. It would have been interesting
to have a similar analysis conducted to the polished sections investigated in this report to see if
a similar tendency exist. To get an actual estimation to whether or not the phenomenon actual
occurs in the ﬁeld and in what kind of environment, a more thorough investigations should have
been conducted.
45
Byg•Dtu 2017
==PAGE:54==
5
|
Conclusion
The air void analyses performed on the paste specimens revealed that hollow ﬂy ash grains are
interpreted as air voids. Paste sample 050, 080, and 100 achieved acceptable spacing factors
exclusively by the eﬀect of the ﬂy ash. After adjusting the air void distribution for the concrete,
the spacing factor for 100 increased with 34 %. Despite the increase being signiﬁcant, none of
the spacing factors exceeds the critical limit. Even though the ﬂy ash does aﬀect the analysis it
can not fully explain the high scaling for concrete containing high contents of ﬂy ash.
The lowest compressive strength was measured on specimen 033p to 38.8 MPa and the highest
was measured on 100p to 61.3 MPa. The high strength at initiation of the frost tests proves that
the content of ﬂy ash does not lead to lower early strength which would makes the specimens
more susceptible to frost damage. This is further backed up by the TGA results which revealed
high degrees of hydration.
In addition the results showed that calcium hydroxide was still
present in all samples.
In the pre-project it was concluded that the salt frost scaling increases with the content of ﬂy ash.
However, the addition of extra air proved to signiﬁcantly decrease the scaling as the mass loss of
100 EA was less than the mass loss of 050p. The result of the mass loss versus the spacing factor
indicates that the critical spacing factor might be lower for concrete with high contents of ﬂy ash.
It was not possible to prove from the freeze/thaw tests why the extra air had such a positive ef-
fect. The tests with NaOH, CaCl2 and changing of the solution did not show improvement to the
scaling nor did it increase the scaling. However, pH measurements showed an increase in the pH
of the 3 % NaCl solution from 8 to approximately 11.5-12.0 after one cycle of freezing and thaw-
ing. The increase in pH indicates leaching of the calcium hydroxide out into the freezing medium.
The petrographic analysis conducted in the pre-project revealed comparable conditions for all
specimens prior to exposure. All thin sections showed empty, micro cracks or ﬁne cracks which
most likely originated from the saw used for cutting the specimens. Petrographic images from 080
and 100 after exposure revealed a low content of calcium hydroxide whereas 000p showed a high
content. According to the calculations of Ca(OH)2 from the TGA results, 000p should contain
2 times as much calcium hydroxide as 080 and almost 3 times as much as 100. However, as the
analysis hardly showed any content of calcium hydroxide in either 080 and 100 it indicates that
the calcium hydroxide has decomposed which can be linked to both leaching and bicarbonation.
The thin sections of 080 and 100 furthermore showed a porous surface and bicarbonation unlike
000p. From the thin sections it is not possible to evaluate how much the present of bicarbonataion
aﬀects the degree of scaling.
46
==PAGE:55==
CHAPTER 5. CONCLUSION
The scanning microscopic analysis conducted in the pre-project showed formation of bicarboation
for all thin sections containing ﬂy ash. The chemical data extracted was re-evaluated in this
report. It was found that the chemical composition of the surface changes during exposure for
the ﬂy ash concretes where sodium originating from the freezing solution appeared in the paste.
Bicarbonation creates a binder consisting of silica gel and calcium crystals. When the calcium
precipitates from the gel it is not unlikely that the silica gel becomes susceptible to other ions
such as sodium and binds them. The test proves that changes in the surface does occur and if
the state of the surface is compromised the necessary air content might have to increase for the
concrete to stay durable.
47

Pre-project
This chapter contains a short version of the pre-project, including a summary of the experimental
work, the results, and a discussion of the results.
51
==PAGE:60==
Introduction
It is possible to improve a concrete’s environmental proﬁle by replacing part of the cement with
ﬂy ash or another kind of pozzolan.
However, every new concrete type must live up to the
standards regarding safety and durability. Previous freeze/thaw tests, performed according to
DS/EN 12390-9:2012, has shown that concrete with high amounts of ﬂy ash does not pass the
criteria for frost resistance.
Summary of Experimental Work Prior to Master Thesis
In order to investigate possible explanations of the poor frost resistance, ﬁve concretes with
increasing amount of ﬂy ash are cast. The ﬂy ash/cement-ratio are 0.00, 0.33, 0.50, 0.80, and 1.00.
The ﬁrst part of the report deals with the properties important for the scaling resistance, namely
the compressive strength and the air void content and structure. The second part deals with
the freeze/thaw test, which is conducted on specimens exposed with a 3% NaCl solution and
specimens exposed with a 6% urea solution. The purpose with the urea exposed specimens is
to investigate if the scaling can be linked to the type of deicers used during testing. The last
part deals with the microscopic analysis which is used to analyse the conditions prior and after
exposure.
1. Pre-testing to Develop Recipes with Comparable Strength
The strength is an important parameter for the scaling resistance. Thus it is optimal that all
mixes have achieved approximately the same strength at the initiation of the freeze-thaw test.
A trial mix was cast to check the compressive strength for each concrete, the target value being
42 MPa. The developed recipes are summarized in Tab. 1.

The speciﬁc surface and spacing factor for each mix are summarized in Tab. 4. None of the spac-
ing factors exceed the critical spacing factor of 0.20 mm deﬁned accordingly to DS2426. Thus
all specimens can be considered frost resistant. The spacing factor for 000 and 033 are gener-
ally lower than the others which corresponds to the higher air content measured in these samples.
As the air void analysis is partly performed by a machine, preparation faults may not be detected.
Sometimes the air voids may not be fully ﬁlled, some white irregularities in the aggregates may
be analysed as voids or if the ﬂy ash contains large amounts of spheres these might be assessed
as air voids as well. It is not known to which extent this might aﬀect the results. It will most
likely not aﬀect the total air content as the voids will be analysed as small voids and therefore
wont contribute signiﬁcantly to the volume. However, it will aﬀect the spacing factor positively
which could be misleading in relation to the frost resistance.
3. Freeze/Thaw Testing
Half of the freeze/thaw tests are conducted according to DS/EN 12390-9:2012 with exposure to
a 3 % NaCl solution. The other half is conducted in the same way but with exposure to a 6 %
urea solution. The concentration of the urea solution has been calculated so the freezing point
is equal to the freezing point of the NaCl solution. The results after 56 freeze-thaw cycle are
summarised in Tab. 5 and the scaling development is illustrated in Fig. 1. According to
SS 13 72 44, the scaling after 56 cycles may not exceed 0.50 and the acceleration may not exceed
2.0. Thus 080 and 100 fails the criteria for acceptable frost resistance.
