54
==PAGE:63==
Figure 1: Scaling for all mixes.
The trend of the results for specimens subjected to the 3 % NaCl solution is an increase in scaling
with the content of ﬂy ash if the last measurement of mix 080 is neglected. For the 56. cycle the
scaling for 080 exceeds the scaling for 100, which breaks the general trend. The scaling for 033
are somewhat equal to the scaling for 000, whereas the scaling for 050 are signiﬁcantly higher
indicating that the scaling amount start to increase with a ﬂy ash/cement-ratio somewhere
between 0.33 and 0.50.
The curves for 050, 080, and 100 subjected to urea all follow the approximately same curves if
neglecting the last measurement. The same goes for 033 and 000, which also seems to achieve
the same amount of scaling. If comparing the scaling for the urea and NaCl exposed specimens
no signiﬁcant diﬀerence exist for 000 and 033. For 050 the urea exposed specimens achieved a
higher scaling than the NaCl exposed specimens which is opposite to 080 and 100 where the
specimens exposed to NaCl achieved the highest scaling.
If the extensive deterioration of concretes with high amount of ﬂy ash was a result of a chemical
attack between the paste and the NaCl the scaling would have been expected to be signiﬁcantly
reduced when exposing the specimens to an urea solution. Thus it is not the type of chemical
de-icers that is the issue.
During testing, one of the frost cabinets broke down for two days. The sampling aﬀected are the
56 cycle measurement for 033, 050, and 080. The test was prolonged by two days to accommodate
for the breakdown. By looking at these measurements an acceleration of the scaling can be
observed. It can not be excluded that it is somehow related to the breakdown.
4. Microscopic Analyses of Concrete before and after Freeze/Thaw
Exposure
Thin sections are prepared from specimens before exposure to freeze-thaw testing, after 42 cycles
of exposure with urea, and after 42 cycles of exposure with NaCl. For 000 however, thin sections
are made already after 28 cycles of exposure due to time pressure. The sections are investigated
in order to register and quantify the conditions prior to testing and the condition after testing.
55
==PAGE:64==
Petrographic Analysis
Petrographic pictures of the surface prior and after exposure are shown in Fig.
2 and 3 in
ﬂorescent- and double polarised light respectively. General for all concrete mixes prior to ex-
posure, was the presence of empty, surface parallel, micro cracks and ﬁne cracks to a depth of
maximum 0.6 mm. All the surfaces are uneven and shows signs of loss of material. The aggre-
gates which have been cut by the saw are crushed or cracked most likely from damage inﬂicted
when cutting the specimens. Based on the petrographic images, it does not appear to be the
surface conditions which are responsible for the excessive scaling for the concretes containing
high amounts of ﬂy ash. The surfaces after exposure shows scaling to a variate extent. However,
as the surface before exposure already showed loss of material, the degree of scaling can not be
estimated on the petrographic images. No deep cracks have developed and there are no signs of
frost related cracks in the samples.

samples apart from 000 after exposure. The reason for the lack of carbonation for 000 could
either be a diﬀerence in properties such as permeability, chemical composition of the concrete
etc. or due to the shorter exposure time for the concrete samples used for thin sections. The
thin sections for 033 and 050 appears to be the most carbonated samples, possible due to the
lower strength, which also aﬀects the permeability, compared to 080 and 100.

Discussion
The concrete specimens with a ﬂy ash/cement-ratio of 0.00, 0.33, and 0.50 all passed the
freeze-thaw test. For 033 hardly any scaling occurred. If comparing the scaling masses for 033
and 050 it becomes clear that somewhere between a ﬂy ash/cement-ratio of 0.33 and 0.50 the
scaling starts to increase. There was a visible decrease in scaling for both 080 and 100 when
exposing the specimens to an urea solution rather than a NaCl solution. However, the decrease
in scaling was not suﬃcient to pass the scaling criteria, thus the type of de-icers can be ruled
out as the cause oﬀthe extensive scaling.
Both the strength of the specimens, the air content, and air structure was satisfying for all
concrete mixes with regards to the scaling resistance. Even though the strength was signiﬁcantly
higher for 080 and 100 compared to 000, 033, and 050, the scaling was still extensive. If it
is assumed that the spheres in ﬂy ash do not aﬀect the air void analysis signiﬁcantly, the air
content and structure can be ruled out to be the causes of the increased scaling for increasing
amount of ﬂy ash. However, normally the amount of air entraining agent would be expected to
increase for increasing amount of ﬂy ash but in this case it decreases. Nevertheless, does the air
void analysis still show a high amount of ﬁne air voids which normally does not appear naturally
in concrete. This supports the theory of the spheres in ﬂy ash interferes with the air void analysis.
The petrographic analysis and the SEM analysis revealed carbonation in the sample after
exposure. For the polished sections of 033, 050, 080, and 100, popcorn carbonation is visible.
The popcorn carbonation, or bicarbonation, causes the calcium from the paste to precipitate
into small lumps leaving the gel susceptible to other ions.
As other ions is absorbed in the
gel, the properties also changes. Popcorn carbonation is known to de a durability problem for
concrete and could cause increased scaling, but as all concretes contained carbonation to some
extend, it is necessary to have a clear knowledge of how aﬀected each sample is before any
conclusions can be made.
The chemical analysis of the inner hydrated paste prior to exposure showed an increase in
carboaluminium hydrate for increasing amount of ﬂy ash. After exposure with NaCl, Friedel’s
salt was formed for the mixes containing ﬂy ash.
Neither the stability for carboaluminium
hydrate or Friedel’s salt are known, but it can be concluded that the chemical composition
of the inner hydrates of the concrete is both aﬀected by the ﬂy ash and the NaCl. Since all
concretes containing ﬂy ash seems to develop approximately the same amount of Friedel’s salt
it would be unlikely that it is the problem.
Further work could be conducted to evaluate if the sphere shaped ﬂy ash grains aﬀect the
results of the air void structure of the concrete and to which extent it might change the results.
According to the article Eﬀect of calcium hydroxide content on the form, extent, and signiﬁcance
of carbonation written by Thaulow, N. et al, popcorn carbonation is a phenomenon occurring
for high w/c ratios. The results from the SEM analysis show that popcorn carbonation can also
occur for low w/c-ratios. Further work could clarify if the absent of Ca(OH)2, caused by the
presence of ﬂy ash, causes the popcorn carbonation and if the high scaling is a result hereof.
61

determined for 1 g of each clinker mineral, then the masses are determined for the given cement
composition.

B.3
Fly Ash Reaction
From the manufactures it was informed that the ﬂy ash consist of 0.564 % S. The amount of
calcium hydroxide produced from the hydration of 1 g cement is used in the calculations, to see
how much S can react and thus how much ﬂy ash it takes to use up all th calcium hydroxide.
